<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Middle_Ages_sidebar" title="Template:Middle Ages sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Middle_Ages_sidebar" title="Template talk:Middle Ages sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Middle_Ages_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>

<p>The <b>Holy Roman Empire</b> (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><i>Sacrum Imperium Romanum</i></span>; <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Heiliges Römisches Reich</i></span>) was a multi-ethnic complex of territories in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a> that developed during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> and continued until its dissolution in 1806.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[7]</a></sup> The largest territory of the empire after 962 was the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">Kingdom of Germany</a>, though it also came to include the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Arles" title="Kingdom of Arles">Kingdom of Burgundy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Kingdom of Italy (Holy Roman Empire)">Kingdom of Italy</a>, and numerous other territories.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whaley_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whaley-12">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-13">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 25 December 800, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a> crowned the <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_the_Franks" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings of the Franks">Frankish king</a> <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> as <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Emperor</a>, reviving the title in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, more than three centuries after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of the Roman Empire">fall</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>. The title continued in the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">Carolingian family</a> until 888 and from 896 to 899, after which it was contested by the rulers of Italy in a series of civil wars until the death of the last Italian claimant, <a href="/wiki/Berengar_I_of_Italy" title="Berengar I of Italy">Berengar</a>, in 924.</p>
<p>The title was revived in 962 when <a href="/wiki/Otto_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto I">Otto I</a> was crowned emperor, fashioning himself as the successor of Charlemagne<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[11]</a></sup> and beginning a continuous existence of the empire for over eight centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-Gascoigne_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gascoigne-15">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> Some historians refer to the coronation of Charlemagne as the origin of the empire,<sup id="cite_ref-Bryce_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bryce-18">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup> while others prefer the coronation of Otto I as its beginning.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> Scholars generally concur, however, in relating an evolution of the institutions and principles constituting the empire, describing a gradual assumption of the imperial title and role.<sup id="cite_ref-Whaley_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whaley-12">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bryce_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bryce-18">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The precise term "Holy Roman Empire" was not used until the 13th century, but the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Translatio_imperii" title="Translatio imperii">translatio imperii</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[d]</a></sup> the notion that he held supreme power inherited from the emperors of Rome, was fundamental to the prestige of the emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-Whaley_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whaley-12">[9]</a></sup> The office of <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> was traditionally elective, although frequently controlled by dynasties. The German <a href="/wiki/Prince-electors" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince-electors">prince-electors</a>, the highest-ranking noblemen of the empire, usually elected one of their peers as "<a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Romans" title="King of the Romans">King of the Romans</a>", and he would later be crowned emperor by the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a>; the tradition of papal coronations was discontinued in the 16th century. The empire never achieved the extent of political unification formed in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, evolving instead into a decentralized, limited elective monarchy composed of hundreds of <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_in_the_Holy_Roman_Empire" title="List of states in the Holy Roman Empire">sub-units</a>, <a href="/wiki/Principality" title="Principality">principalities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy" title="Duchy">duchies</a>, <a href="/wiki/County" title="County">counties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_Imperial_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Imperial City">Free Imperial Cities</a>, and other domains.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-13">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup> The power of the emperor was limited, and while the various princes, lords, bishops, and cities of the empire were vassals who owed the emperor their allegiance, they also possessed an extent of privileges that gave them <i>de facto</i> independence within their territories. Emperor <a href="/wiki/Francis_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Francis II, Holy Roman Emperor">Francis II</a> dissolved the empire on 6 August 1806, after the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine" title="Confederation of the Rhine">Confederation of the Rhine</a> by <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In various languages the Holy Roman Empire was known as: <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><i>Sacrum Imperium Romanum</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Heiliges Römisches Reich</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <span lang="it" xml:lang="it"><i>Sacro Romano Impero</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>: <span lang="cs" xml:lang="cs"><i>Svatá říše římská</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Slovene_language" title="Slovene language">Slovene</a>: <span lang="sl" xml:lang="sl"><i>Sveto rimsko cesarstvo</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <span lang="nl" xml:lang="nl"><i>Heilige Roomse Rijk</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i>Saint-Empire romain</i></span> (before Otto I), <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i>Saint-Empire romain germanique</i></span> (by Otto I).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup> Before 1157, the realm was merely referred to as the Roman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-wilson_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-25">[21]</a></sup> The term <i>sacrum</i> ("holy", in the sense of "consecrated") in connection with the medieval Roman Empire was used beginning in 1157 under <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I Barbarossa</a> ("Holy Empire"): the term was added to reflect Frederick's ambition to dominate Italy and the Papacy.<sup id="cite_ref-Whaley17_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whaley17-26">[22]</a></sup> The form "Holy Roman Empire" is attested from 1254 onward.<sup id="cite_ref-Peter_Moraw_1999_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Moraw_1999-27">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a decree following the 1512 <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Diet_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Imperial Diet (Holy Roman Empire)">Diet</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, the name was changed to <b>Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Heiliges Römisches Reich Deutscher Nation</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><i>Imperium Romanum Sacrum Nationis Germanicæ</i></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GT-DEX-1000-01_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GT-DEX-1000-01-29">[25]</a></sup> a form first used in a document in 1474.<sup id="cite_ref-Whaley17_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whaley17-26">[22]</a></sup> The new title was adopted partly because the Empire had lost most of its Italian and Burgundian (<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Arles" title="Kingdom of Arles">Kingdom of Arles</a>) territories by the late 15th century,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup> but also to emphasize the new importance of the German <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Estate" title="Imperial Estate">Imperial Estates</a> in ruling the Empire due to the Imperial Reform.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup> By the end of the 18th century, the term "Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation" had fallen out of official use. Besides, contradicting the traditional view concerning that designation, Hermann Weisert has stated in a study on imperial titulature that, despite the claim of many textbooks, the name <i>Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation</i> never had an official status and points out that documents were thirty times as likely to omit the national suffix as include it.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a famous assessment of the name, the French <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> remarked sardonically: "This agglomeration which was called and which still calls itself the Holy Roman Empire was in no way holy, nor Roman, nor an empire."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carolingian_forerunners">Carolingian forerunners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Carolingian forerunners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a></div>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Roman</a> power in <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> declined during the 5th century, local Germanic tribes assumed control.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[30]</a></sup> In the late 5th and early 6th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_dynasty" title="Merovingian dynasty">Merovingians</a>, under <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a> and his successors, consolidated <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Frankish</a> tribes and extended hegemony over others to gain control of northern Gaul and the middle <a href="/wiki/Rhine_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhine river">Rhine river</a> valley region.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[32]</a></sup> By the middle of the 8th century, however, the Merovingians had been reduced to figureheads, and the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">Carolingians</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a>, had become the <i>de facto</i> rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> In 751, Martel’s son <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin</a> became King of the Franks, and later gained the sanction of the Pope.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EB-Pippin_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB-Pippin-39">[35]</a></sup> The Carolingians would maintain a close alliance with the Papacy.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 768 Pepin’s son <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> became King of the Franks and began an extensive expansion of the realm. He eventually incorporated the territories of present-day France, Germany, northern Italy, and beyond, linking the Frankish kingdom with Papal lands.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup> On Christmas Day of 800, Pope Leo III crowned Charlemagne emperor, restoring the title in the West for the first time in over three centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Charlemagne died in 814, the imperial crown passed to his son, <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Pious" title="Louis the Pious">Louis the Pious</a>. Upon Louis' death in 840, it passed to his son <a href="/wiki/Lothair_I" title="Lothair I">Lothair</a>, who had been his co-ruler. By this point the territory of Charlemagne had been divided into several territories, and over the course of the later ninth century the title of Emperor was disputed by the Carolingian rulers of <a href="/wiki/Western_Francia" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Francia">Western Francia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Francia" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Francia">Eastern Francia</a>, with first the western king (<a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bald" title="Charles the Bald">Charles the Bald</a>) and then the eastern (<a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Fat" title="Charles the Fat">Charles the Fat</a>), who briefly reunited the Empire, attaining the prize.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After the death of Charles the Fat in 888, however, the Carolingian Empire broke apart, and was never restored. According to <a href="/wiki/Regino_of_Pr%C3%BCm" title="Regino of Prüm">Regino of Prüm</a>, the parts of the realm "spewed forth kinglets", and each part elected a kinglet "from its own bowels".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> After the death of Charles the Fat, those crowned emperor by the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">pope</a> controlled only territories in Italy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The last such emperor was <a href="/wiki/Berengar_I_of_Italy" title="Berengar I of Italy">Berengar I of Italy</a>, who died in 924.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Around 900, autonomous <a href="/wiki/Stem_duchy" title="Stem duchy">stem duchies</a> (<a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Franconia" title="Duchy of Franconia">Franconia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Bavaria" title="Duchy of Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Swabia" title="Duchy of Swabia">Swabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Saxony" title="Duchy of Saxony">Saxony</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lotharingia#Duchy_of_Lotharingia" title="Lotharingia">Lotharingia</a>) reemerged in East Francia. After the Carolingian king <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Child" title="Louis the Child">Louis the Child</a> died without issue in 911, East Francia did not turn to the Carolingian ruler of West Francia to take over the realm but instead elected one of the dukes, <a href="/wiki/Conrad_of_Franconia" class="mw-redirect" title="Conrad of Franconia">Conrad of Franconia</a>, as <i>Rex Francorum Orientalium</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-46">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:117</sup> On his deathbed, Conrad yielded the crown to his main rival, <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Fowler" title="Henry the Fowler">Henry the Fowler</a> of Saxony (r. 919–36), who was elected king at the Diet of <a href="/wiki/Fritzlar" title="Fritzlar">Fritzlar</a> in 919.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-46">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:118</sup> Henry reached a truce with the raiding <a href="/wiki/Magyars" class="mw-redirect" title="Magyars">Magyars</a>, and in 933 he won a first victory against them in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Riade" title="Battle of Riade">Battle of Riade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-46">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:121</sup></p>
<p>Henry died in 936, but his descendants, the <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_dynasty" title="Ottonian dynasty">Liudolfing (or Ottonian) dynasty</a>, would continue to rule the Eastern kingdom for roughly a century. Upon Henry the Fowler's death, <a href="/wiki/Otto_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto I, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto</a>, his son and designated successor,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup> was elected King in <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a> in 936.<sup id="cite_ref-magill_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-magill-48">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:706</sup> He overcame a series of revolts from an elder brother and from several dukes. After that, the king managed to control the appointment of dukes and often also employed bishops in administrative affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-cantor_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cantor-49">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:212–13</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HRR.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/HRR.gif/250px-HRR.gif" width="250" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/HRR.gif/375px-HRR.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/HRR.gif/500px-HRR.gif 2x" data-file-width="687" data-file-height="579" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HRR.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Holy Roman Empire from 962 to 1806</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 951, Otto came to the aid of <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_of_Italy" title="Adelaide of Italy">Adelaide</a>, the widowed queen of Italy, defeating her enemies, marrying her, and taking control over Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-cantor_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cantor-49">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:214–15</sup> In 955, Otto won a decisive victory over the <a href="/wiki/Magyars" class="mw-redirect" title="Magyars">Magyars</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lechfeld" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Lechfeld">Battle of Lechfeld</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-magill_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-magill-48">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:707</sup> In 962, Otto was crowned Emperor by <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XII" title="Pope John XII">Pope John XII</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-magill_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-magill-48">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:707</sup> thus intertwining the affairs of the German kingdom with those of Italy and the Papacy. Otto's coronation as Emperor marked the German kings as successors to the Empire of Charlemagne, which through the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Translatio_imperii" title="Translatio imperii">translatio imperii</a></i>, also made them consider themselves as successors to Ancient Rome.</p>
<p>The kingdom had no permanent capital city.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup> Kings traveled between residences (called <a href="/wiki/Kaiserpfalz" title="Kaiserpfalz">Kaiserpfalz</a>) to discharge affairs. However, each king preferred certain places; in Otto's case, this was the city of <a href="/wiki/Magdeburg" title="Magdeburg">Magdeburg</a>. Kingship continued to be transferred by election, but Kings often ensured their own sons were elected during their lifetimes, enabling them to keep the crown for their families. This only changed after the end of the Salian dynasty in the 12th century.</p>
<p>In 963, Otto deposed the current pope John XII and chose <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_VIII" title="Pope Leo VIII">Pope Leo VIII</a> as the new pope (although John XII and Leo VIII both claimed the papacy until 964, when John XII died). This also renewed the conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine Emperor">Eastern Emperor in Constantinople</a>, especially after Otto's son <a href="/wiki/Otto_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto II, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto II</a> (r. 967–83) adopted the designation <i>imperator Romanorum</i>. Still, Otto formed marital ties with the east when he married the Byzantine princess <a href="/wiki/Theophanu" title="Theophanu">Theophanu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-magill_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-magill-48">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:708</sup> Their son, <a href="/wiki/Otto_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto III, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto III</a>, came to the throne only three years old, and was subjected to a power struggle and series of regencies until his age of majority in 994. Up to that time, he had remained in Germany, while a deposed Duke, <a href="/wiki/Crescentius_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Crescentius II">Crescentius II</a>, ruled over Rome and part of Italy, ostensibly in his stead.</p>
<p>In 996 Otto III appointed his cousin <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_V" title="Pope Gregory V">Gregory V</a> the first German Pope.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[47]</a></sup> A foreign pope and foreign papal officers were seen with suspicion by Roman nobles, who were led by <a href="/wiki/Crescentius_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Crescentius II">Crescentius II</a> to revolt. <a href="/wiki/Otto_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto III">Otto III</a>'s former mentor <a href="/wiki/Antipope_John_XVI" title="Antipope John XVI">Antipope John XVI</a> briefly held Rome, until the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> seized the city.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Otto died young in 1002, and was succeeded by his cousin <a href="/wiki/Henry_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry II, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry II</a>, who focused on Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-cantor_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cantor-49">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:215–17</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Henry_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry II, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry II</a> died in 1024 and <a href="/wiki/Conrad_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Conrad II, Holy Roman Emperor">Conrad II</a>, first of the <a href="/wiki/Salian" class="mw-redirect" title="Salian">Salian Dynasty</a>, was elected king only after some debate among dukes and nobles. This group eventually developed into the college of <a href="/wiki/Prince-elector" title="Prince-elector">Electors</a>.</p>
<p>The Holy Roman Empire became eventually composed of four kingdoms and numerous other territories. The kingdoms were:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">Kingdom of Germany</a> (part of the empire since 962),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Kingdom of Italy (Holy Roman Empire)">Kingdom of Italy</a> (since 962 until 1648),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a> (since 1002 as the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Bohemia" title="Duchy of Bohemia">Duchy of Bohemia</a> and raised to a kingdom in 1198),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Arles" title="Kingdom of Arles">Kingdom of Burgundy</a> (since 1032 until 1378).</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Middle_Ages">High Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: High Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Investiture_controversy">Investiture controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Investiture controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Kings often employed bishops in administrative affairs and often determined who would be appointed to ecclesiastical offices.<sup id="cite_ref-Barraclough_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barraclough-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:101–134</sup> In the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Cluniac_Reforms" title="Cluniac Reforms">Cluniac Reforms</a>, this involvement was increasingly seen as inappropriate by the Papacy. The reform-minded <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_VII" title="Pope Gregory VII">Pope Gregory VII</a> was determined to oppose such practices, which led to the <a href="/wiki/Investiture_Controversy" title="Investiture Controversy">Investiture Controversy</a> with King <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry IV</a> (r. 1056–1106).<sup id="cite_ref-Barraclough_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barraclough-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:101–134</sup> He repudiated the Pope's interference and persuaded his bishops to excommunicate the Pope, whom he famously addressed by his born name "Hildebrand", rather than his regnal name "Pope Gregory VII".<sup id="cite_ref-Barraclough_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barraclough-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:109</sup> The Pope, in turn, excommunicated the king, declared him deposed, and dissolved the oaths of loyalty made to Henry.<sup id="cite_ref-Gascoigne_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gascoigne-15">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barraclough_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barraclough-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:109</sup> The king found himself with almost no political support and was forced to make the famous <a href="/wiki/Walk_to_Canossa" class="mw-redirect" title="Walk to Canossa">Walk to Canossa</a> in 1077,<sup id="cite_ref-Barraclough_53-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barraclough-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:122–24</sup> by which he achieved a lifting of the excommunication at the price of humiliation. Meanwhile, the German princes had elected another king, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_of_Swabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Rudolf of Swabia">Rudolf of Swabia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Barraclough_53-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barraclough-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:123</sup> Henry managed to defeat him but was subsequently confronted with more uprisings, renewed excommunication, and even the rebellion of his sons. After his death, his second son, <a href="/wiki/Henry_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry V, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry V</a>, reached an agreement with the Pope and the bishops in the 1122 <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_Worms" title="Concordat of Worms">Concordat of Worms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Barraclough_53-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barraclough-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:123–34</sup> The political power of the Empire was maintained, but the conflict had demonstrated the limits of the ruler's power, especially in regard to the Church, and it robbed the king of the sacral status he had previously enjoyed. The Pope and the German princes had surfaced as major players in the political system of the empire.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_Roman_Empire_under_Hohenstaufen_dynasty">Holy Roman Empire under Hohenstaufen dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Holy Roman Empire under Hohenstaufen dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg/250px-Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg.png" width="250" height="325" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg/375px-Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg/500px-Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="8291" data-file-height="10793" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Hohenstaufen-ruled Holy Roman Empire and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Kingdom of Sicily</a>. Imperial and directly held Hohenstaufen lands in the Empire are shown in bright yellow.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When the Salian dynasty ended with Henry V's death in 1125, the princes chose not to elect the next of kin, but rather <a href="/wiki/Lothair_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Lothair III, Holy Roman Emperor">Lothair</a>, the moderately powerful but already old Duke of Saxony. When he died in 1137, the princes again aimed to check royal power; accordingly they did not elect Lothair's favoured heir, his son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Henry_X,_Duke_of_Bavaria" title="Henry X, Duke of Bavaria">Henry the Proud</a> of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Welf" title="House of Welf">Welf</a> family, but <a href="/wiki/Conrad_III_of_Germany" title="Conrad III of Germany">Conrad III</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hohenstaufen" title="Hohenstaufen">Hohenstaufen</a> family, the grandson of Emperor Henry IV and thus a nephew of Emperor Henry V. This led to over a century of strife between the two houses. Conrad ousted the Welfs from their possessions, but after his death in 1152, his nephew <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I "Barbarossa"</a> succeeded him and made peace with the Welfs, restoring his cousin <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Lion" title="Henry the Lion">Henry the Lion</a> to his – albeit diminished – possessions.</p>
<p>The Hohenstaufen rulers increasingly lent land to <i>ministerialia</i>, formerly non-free service men, who Frederick hoped would be more reliable than dukes. Initially used mainly for war services, this new class of people would form the basis for the later <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Knight" title="Imperial Knight">knights</a>, another basis of imperial power. A further important constitutional move at Roncaglia was the establishment of a new peace mechanism for the entire empire, the <a href="/wiki/Landfrieden" title="Landfrieden">Landfrieden</a>, with the first imperial one being issued in 1103 under <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry IV</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> This was an attempt to abolish private feuds, between the many dukes and other people, and to tie the Emperor's subordinates to a legal system of jurisdiction and public prosecution of criminal acts – a predecessor of the modern concept of "<a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>". Another new concept of the time was the systematic foundation of new cities by the Emperor and by the local dukes. These were partly caused by the explosion in population, and they also concentrated economic power at strategic locations. Before this, cities had only existed in the form of old Roman foundations or older bishoprics. Cities that were founded in the 12th century include <a href="/wiki/Freiburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Freiburg">Freiburg</a>, possibly the economic model for many later cities, and <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I</a>, also called Frederick Barbarossa, was crowned Emperor in 1155. He emphasized the "Romanness" of the empire, partly in an attempt to justify the power of the Emperor independent of the (now strengthened) Pope. An imperial assembly at the fields of Roncaglia in 1158 reclaimed imperial rights in reference to <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a>. Imperial rights had been referred to as <i>regalia</i> since the Investiture Controversy, but were enumerated for the first time at Roncaglia. This comprehensive list included public roads, tariffs, coining, collecting punitive fees, and the investiture or seating and unseating of office holders. These rights were now explicitly rooted in Roman Law, a far-reaching constitutional act.</p>
<p>Frederick's policies were primarily directed at Italy, where he clashed with the increasingly wealthy and free-minded cities of the north, especially <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>. He also embroiled himself in another conflict with the Papacy by supporting a candidate elected by a minority against <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_III" title="Pope Alexander III">Pope Alexander III</a> (1159–81). Frederick supported a succession of <a href="/wiki/Antipope" title="Antipope">antipopes</a> before finally making peace with Alexander in 1177. In Germany, the Emperor had repeatedly protected Henry the Lion against complaints by rival princes or cities (especially in the cases of <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> and <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="Lübeck">Lübeck</a>). Henry gave only lackluster support to Frederick's policies, and in a critical situation during the Italian wars, Henry refused the Emperor's plea for military support. After returning to Germany, an embittered Frederick opened proceedings against the Duke, resulting in a public ban and the confiscation of all his territories. In 1190, Frederick participated in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third Crusade</a> and died in the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Kingdom_of_Cilicia" title="Armenian Kingdom of Cilicia">Armenian Kingdom of Cilicia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HRE-at-its-peak.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/HRE-at-its-peak.jpg/300px-HRE-at-its-peak.jpg" width="300" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/HRE-at-its-peak.jpg/450px-HRE-at-its-peak.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/HRE-at-its-peak.jpg/600px-HRE-at-its-peak.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2276" data-file-height="1456" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HRE-at-its-peak.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Holy Roman Empire at its maximal extent, in the 13th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the Hohenstaufen period, German princes facilitated a successful, peaceful <a href="/wiki/Ostsiedlung" title="Ostsiedlung">eastward settlement</a> of lands that were uninhabited or inhabited sparsely by <a href="/wiki/West_Slavs" title="West Slavs">West Slavs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">German speaking</a> farmers, traders, and craftsmen from the western part of the Empire, both Christians and Jews, moved into these areas. The gradual <a href="/wiki/Germanization" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanization">Germanization</a> of these lands was a complex phenomenon that should not be interpreted in the biased terms of 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>. The eastward settlement expanded the influence of the empire to include <a href="/wiki/Pomerania" title="Pomerania">Pomerania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a>, as did the intermarriage of the local, still mostly Slavic, rulers with German spouses. The <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a> were invited to <a href="/wiki/Prussia_(region)" title="Prussia (region)">Prussia</a> by Duke <a href="/wiki/Konrad_of_Masovia" class="mw-redirect" title="Konrad of Masovia">Konrad of Masovia</a> to Christianize the <a href="/wiki/Old_Prussians" title="Old Prussians">Prussians</a> in 1226. The <a href="/wiki/Monastic_state_of_the_Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Monastic state of the Teutonic Knights">monastic state of the Teutonic Order</a> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Deutschordensstaat</i></span>) and its later German successor state of <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> were, however, never part of the Holy Roman Empire.</p>
<p>Under the son and successor of Frederick Barbarossa, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry VI, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry VI</a>, the Hohenstaufen dynasty reached its apex. Henry added the Norman kingdom of Sicily to his domains, held English king <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard the Lionheart</a> captive, and aimed to establish a hereditary monarchy when he died in 1197. As his son, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick II</a>, though already elected king, was still a small child and living in Sicily, German princes chose to elect an adult king, resulting in the dual election of Frederick Barbarossa's youngest son <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Swabia" title="Philip of Swabia">Philip of Swabia</a> and Henry the Lion's son <a href="/wiki/Otto_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto of Brunswick</a>, who competed for the crown. Otto prevailed for a while after Philip was murdered in a private squabble in 1208, until he began to also claim Sicily.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_(1200-1350).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg/220px-War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg.png" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg/330px-War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg/440px-War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_(1200-1350).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Reichssturmfahne" class="mw-redirect" title="Reichssturmfahne">Reichssturmfahne</a></i>, a military <a href="/wiki/Banner" title="Banner">banner</a> during the 13th and early 14th centuries.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Pope Innocent III</a>, who feared the threat posed by a union of the empire and Sicily, now supported Frederick II, who marched to Germany and defeated Otto. After his victory, Frederick did not act upon his promise to keep the two realms separate. Though he had made his son Henry king of Sicily before marching on Germany, he still reserved real political power for himself. This continued after Frederick was crowned Emperor in 1220. Fearing Frederick's concentration of power, the Pope finally excommunicated the Emperor. Another point of contention was the crusade, which Frederick had promised but repeatedly postponed. Now, although excommunicated, Frederick led the <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Crusade" title="Sixth Crusade">Sixth Crusade</a> in 1228, which ended in negotiations and a temporary restoration of the Kingdom of Jerusalem.</p>
<p>Despite his imperial claims, Frederick's rule was a major turning point towards the disintegration of central rule in the Empire. While concentrated on establishing a modern, centralized state in Sicily, he was mostly absent from Germany and issued far-reaching privileges to Germany's secular and ecclesiastical princes: In the 1220 <i><a href="/wiki/Confoederatio_cum_principibus_ecclesiasticis" title="Confoederatio cum principibus ecclesiasticis">Confoederatio cum principibus ecclesiasticis</a>,</i> Frederick gave up a number of <i>regalia</i> in favour of the bishops, among them tariffs, coining, and fortification. The 1232 <i><a href="/wiki/Statutum_in_favorem_principum" title="Statutum in favorem principum">Statutum in favorem principum</a></i> mostly extended these privileges to secular territories. Although many of these privileges had existed earlier, they were now granted globally, and once and for all, to allow the German princes to maintain order north of the Alps while Frederick concentrated on Italy. The 1232 document marked the first time that the German dukes were called <i>domini terræ,</i> owners of their lands, a remarkable change in terminology as well.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Bohemia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png/220px-Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png/330px-Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png/440px-Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png 2x" data-file-width="823" data-file-height="821" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lands_of_the_Bohemian_Crown" title="Lands of the Bohemian Crown">Lands of the Bohemian Crown</a> since the reign of Holy Roman Emperor <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles IV</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a> was a significant regional power during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. In 1212, King <a href="/wiki/Ottokar_I_of_Bohemia" title="Ottokar I of Bohemia">Ottokar I</a> (bearing the title "king" since 1198) extracted a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Bull_of_Sicily" title="Golden Bull of Sicily">Golden Bull of Sicily</a> (a formal edict) from the emperor <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick II</a>, confirming the royal title for Ottokar and his descendants and the Duchy of Bohemia was raised to a kingdom. Bohemian kings would be exempt from all future obligations to the Holy Roman Empire except for participation in the imperial councils. <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles IV</a> set <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> to be the seat of the Holy Roman Emperor.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interregnum">Interregnum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Interregnum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After the death of Frederick II in 1250, the German kingdom was divided between his son <a href="/wiki/Conrad_IV_of_Germany" title="Conrad IV of Germany">Conrad IV</a> (died 1254) and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-king" title="Anti-king">anti-king</a>, <a href="/wiki/Count_William_II_of_Holland" class="mw-redirect" title="Count William II of Holland">William of Holland</a> (died 1256). Conrad's death was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Interregnum" title="Interregnum">Interregnum</a>, during which no king could achieve universal recognition, allowing the princes to consolidate their holdings and become even more independent rulers. After 1257, the crown was contested between <a href="/wiki/Richard,_1st_Earl_of_Cornwall" title="Richard, 1st Earl of Cornwall">Richard of Cornwall</a>, who was supported by the Guelph party, and <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_X_of_Castile" title="Alfonso X of Castile">Alfonso X of Castile</a>, who was recognized by the Hohenstaufen party but never set foot on German soil. After Richard's death in 1273, the Interregnum ended with the unanimous election of <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_I_of_Germany" title="Rudolf I of Germany">Rudolf I of Germany</a>, a minor pro-Staufen count.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_political_structure">Changes in political structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Changes in political structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_commune" title="Medieval commune">Medieval commune</a>, <a href="/wiki/St%C3%A4dtebund" class="mw-redirect" title="Städtebund">Städtebund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperial_immediacy" title="Imperial immediacy">Imperial immediacy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Feudalism_in_the_Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Feudalism in the Holy Roman Empire">Feudalism in the Holy Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Schedelsche_Weltchronik_Struktur_des_Reiches.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Schedelsche_Weltchronik_Struktur_des_Reiches.jpg/220px-Schedelsche_Weltchronik_Struktur_des_Reiches.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Schedelsche_Weltchronik_Struktur_des_Reiches.jpg/330px-Schedelsche_Weltchronik_Struktur_des_Reiches.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Schedelsche_Weltchronik_Struktur_des_Reiches.jpg/440px-Schedelsche_Weltchronik_Struktur_des_Reiches.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2179" data-file-height="1612" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Schedelsche_Weltchronik_Struktur_des_Reiches.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An illustration from <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Chronicle" title="Nuremberg Chronicle">Schedelsche Weltchronik</a> depicting the structure of the Reich: The Holy Roman Emperor is sitting; on his right are three ecclesiastics; on his left are four secular electors.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the 13th century, a general structural change in how land was administered prepared the shift of political power towards the rising <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a> at the expense of aristocratic <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> that would characterize the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>. Instead of personal duties, money increasingly became the common means to represent economic value in agriculture. Peasants were increasingly required to pay tribute for their lands. The concept of "property" began to replace more ancient forms of jurisdiction, although they were still very much tied together. In the territories (not at the level of the Empire), power became increasingly bundled: Whoever owned the land had jurisdiction, from which other powers derived. It is important to note, however, that jurisdiction at this time did not include legislation, which virtually did not exist until well into the 15th century. Court practice heavily relied on traditional customs or rules described as customary.</p>
<p>During this time territories began to transform into the predecessors of modern states. The process varied greatly among the various lands and was most advanced in those territories that were most identical to the lands of the old Germanic tribes, <i>e.g.</i> Bavaria. It was slower in those scattered territories that were founded through imperial privileges.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Middle_Ages">Late Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Late Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pomerania_during_the_Late_Middle_Ages" title="Pomerania during the Late Middle Ages">Pomerania during the Late Middle Ages</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_the_territories_after_the_Hohenstaufens">Rise of the territories after the Hohenstaufens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Rise of the territories after the Hohenstaufens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg/250px-Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg" width="250" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg/375px-Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg/500px-Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3586" data-file-height="2792" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Double-headed eagle with coats of arms of individual states, symbol of the Holy Roman Empire (painting from 1510)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The difficulties in electing the king eventually led to the emergence of a fixed college of <a href="/wiki/Prince-elector" title="Prince-elector">prince-electors</a> (<i>Kurfürsten</i>), whose composition and procedures were set forth in the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Bull_of_1356" title="Golden Bull of 1356">Golden Bull of 1356</a>, which remained valid until 1806. This development probably best symbolizes the emerging duality between emperor and realm (<i>Kaiser und Reich</i>), which were no longer considered identical. The Golden Bull also set forth the system for election of the Holy Roman Emperor. The emperor now was to be elected by a majority rather than by consent of all seven electors. For electors the title became hereditary, and they were given the right to mint coins and to exercise jurisdiction. Also their sons were to know the imperial languages – <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-langues_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-langues-3">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The shift in power away from the emperor is also revealed in the way the post-Hohenstaufen kings attempted to sustain their power. Earlier, the Empire's strength (and finances) greatly relied on the Empire's own lands, the so-called <i>Reichsgut</i>, which always belonged to the king of the day and included many Imperial Cities. After the 13th century, the relevance of the <i>Reichsgut</i> faded, even though some parts of it did remain until the Empire's end in 1806. Instead, the <i>Reichsgut</i> was increasingly pawned to local dukes, sometimes to raise money for the Empire, but more frequently to reward faithful duty or as an attempt to establish control over the dukes. The direct governance of the <i>Reichsgut</i> no longer matched the needs of either the king or the dukes.</p>
<p>The kings beginning with <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_I_of_Germany" title="Rudolf I of Germany">Rudolf I of Germany</a> increasingly relied on the lands of their respective dynasties to support their power. In contrast with the <i>Reichsgut</i>, which was mostly scattered and difficult to administer, these territories were relatively compact and thus easier to control. In 1282, Rudolf I thus lent Austria and <a href="/wiki/Styria_(duchy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Styria (duchy)">Styria</a> to his own sons. In 1312, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry VII, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry VII</a> of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Luxembourg" title="House of Luxembourg">House of Luxembourg</a> was crowned as the first Holy Roman Emperor since Frederick II. After him all kings and emperors relied on the lands of their own family (<i>Hausmacht</i>): <a href="/wiki/Louis_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Louis IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Louis IV</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wittelsbach" class="mw-redirect" title="Wittelsbach">Wittelsbach</a> (king 1314, emperor 1328–47) relied on his lands in Bavaria; <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles IV</a> of Luxembourg, the grandson of Henry VII, drew strength from his own lands in Bohemia. Interestingly, it was thus increasingly in the king's own interest to strengthen the power of the territories, since the king profited from such a benefit in his own lands as well.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_reform">Imperial reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Imperial reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vehmic_court" title="Vehmic court">Vehmic court</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HRR_1400.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/HRR_1400.png/250px-HRR_1400.png" width="250" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/HRR_1400.png/375px-HRR_1400.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/HRR_1400.png/500px-HRR_1400.png 2x" data-file-width="3715" data-file-height="3966" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HRR_1400.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Holy Roman Empire in 1400</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The "constitution" of the Empire still remained largely unsettled at the beginning of the 15th century. Although some procedures and institutions had been fixed, for example by the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Bull_of_1356" title="Golden Bull of 1356">Golden Bull of 1356</a>, the rules of how the king, the electors, and the other dukes should cooperate in the Empire much depended on the personality of the respective king. It therefore proved somewhat damaging that <a href="/wiki/Sigismund,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Sigismund, Holy Roman Emperor">Sigismund of Luxemburg</a> (king 1410, emperor 1433–1437) and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick III, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick III of Habsburg</a> (king 1440, emperor 1452–1493) neglected the old core lands of the empire and mostly resided in their own lands. Without the presence of the king, the old institution of the <i><a href="/wiki/Hoftag" title="Hoftag">Hoftag</a></i>, the assembly of the realm's leading men, deteriorated. The <i><a href="/wiki/Imperial_Diet_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Imperial Diet (Holy Roman Empire)">Imperial Diet</a></i> as a legislative organ of the Empire did not exist at that time. The dukes often conducted feuds against each other – feuds that, more often than not, escalated into local wars.</p>
<p>Simultaneously, the Catholic Church experienced crises of its own, with wide-reaching effects in the Empire. The conflict between several papal claimants (two <a href="/wiki/Anti-popes" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-popes">anti-popes</a> and the "legitimate" <a href="/wiki/Papacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Papacy">Pope</a>) ended only with the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">Council of Constance</a> (1414–1418); after 1419 the Papacy directed much of its energy to suppressing the <a href="/wiki/Hussites" title="Hussites">Hussites</a>. The medieval idea of unifying all <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> into a single political entity, with the Church and the Empire as its leading institutions, began to decline.</p>
<p>With these drastic changes, much discussion emerged in the 15th century about the Empire itself. Rules from the past no longer adequately described the structure of the time, and a reinforcement of earlier <i>Landfrieden</i> was urgently needed. During this time, the concept of "reform" emerged, in the original sense of the Latin verb <i>re-formare</i> – to regain an earlier shape that had been lost.</p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick III, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick III</a> needed the dukes to finance a <a href="/wiki/Austrian-Hungarian_War_(1477%E2%80%931488)" class="mw-redirect" title="Austrian-Hungarian War (1477–1488)">war against Hungary in 1486</a>, and at the same time had his son (later <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">Maximilian I</a>) elected king, he faced a demand from the united dukes for their participation in an Imperial Court. For the first time, the assembly of the electors and other dukes was now called the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Diet_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Imperial Diet (Holy Roman Empire)">Imperial Diet</a> (German <i>Reichstag</i>) (to be joined by the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Free_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Free City">Imperial Free Cities</a> later). While Frederick refused, his more conciliatory son finally convened the <a href="/wiki/Diet_at_Worms_in_1495" class="mw-redirect" title="Diet at Worms in 1495">Diet at Worms in 1495</a>, after his father's death in 1493. Here, the king and the dukes agreed on four bills, commonly referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Reform" title="Imperial Reform"><i>Reichsreform</i> (Imperial Reform)</a>: a set of legal acts to give the disintegrating Empire some structure. For example, this act produced the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Circle_Estates" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Circle Estates">Imperial Circle Estates</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Reichskammergericht" title="Reichskammergericht">Reichskammergericht</a></i> (Imperial Chamber Court), institutions that would – to a degree – persist until the end of the Empire in 1806.</p>
<p>However, it took a few more decades for the new regulation to gain universal acceptance and for the new court to begin to function effectively; only in 1512 would the Imperial Circles be finalized. The King also made sure that his own court, the <i>Reichshofrat</i>, continued to operate in parallel to the <i>Reichskammergericht</i>. Also in 1512 the Empire received its new title, the <i>Heiliges Römisches Reich Deutscher Nation</i> ("Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation").</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation_and_Renaissance">Reformation and Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Reformation and Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/German_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="German Reformation">German Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_Renaissance" title="German Renaissance">German Renaissance</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carta_itineraria_europae_1520_waldseemueller_watermarked.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Carta_itineraria_europae_1520_waldseemueller_watermarked.jpg/250px-Carta_itineraria_europae_1520_waldseemueller_watermarked.jpg" width="250" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Carta_itineraria_europae_1520_waldseemueller_watermarked.jpg/375px-Carta_itineraria_europae_1520_waldseemueller_watermarked.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Carta_itineraria_europae_1520_waldseemueller_watermarked.jpg/500px-Carta_itineraria_europae_1520_waldseemueller_watermarked.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4500" data-file-height="3402" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carta_itineraria_europae_1520_waldseemueller_watermarked.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carta itineraria europae (by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Waldseem%C3%BCller" title="Martin Waldseemüller">Waldseemüller</a>, 1520 dedicated to Emperor <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V.)</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1516, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">Ferdinand II of Aragon</a>, grandfather of the future Holy Roman Emperor <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V</a>, died.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup> Due to a combination of (1) the traditions of dynastic succession in Aragon, which permitted maternal inheritance with no precedence for female rule; (2) the insanity of Charles's mother, <a href="/wiki/Joanna_of_Castile" title="Joanna of Castile">Joanna of Castile</a>; and (3) the insistence by his remaining grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Maximilian I of the Holy Roman Empire">Maximilian I</a>, that he take up his royal titles, Charles initiated his reign in Castile and Aragon, a union which evolved into <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish Crown">Spain</a>, in conjunction with his mother. This ensured for the first time that all the realms of what is now Spain would be united by one monarch under one nascent Spanish crown. The founding territories retained their separate governance codes and laws. In 1519, already reigning as <i>Carlos I</i> in Spain, Charles took up the imperial title as <i>Karl V</i>. The balance (and imbalance) between these separate inheritances would be defining elements of his reign, and would ensure that personal union between the Spanish and German crowns would be short-lived. The latter would end up going to a more junior branch of the Habsburgs in the person of Charles's brother <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand I, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand</a>, while the senior branch continued rule in Spain and in the Burgundian inheritance in the person of Charles's son, <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II of Spain</a>.</p>
<p>In addition to conflicts between his Spanish and German inheritances, conflicts of religion would be another source of tension during the reign of Charles V. Before Charles's reign in the Holy Roman Empire began, in 1517, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> launched what would later be known as the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>. At this time, many local dukes saw it as a chance to oppose the hegemony of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V</a>. The empire then became fatally divided along religious lines, with the north, the east, and many of the major cities – <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a> – becoming <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> while the southern and western regions largely remained <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Catholic</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baroque_period">Baroque period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Baroque period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Germany_in_the_early_modern_period" title="Germany in the early modern period">Germany in the early modern period</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pomerania_during_the_Early_Modern_Age" title="Pomerania during the Early Modern Age">Pomerania during the Early Modern Age</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Baroque_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Baroque period">Baroque period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Union" title="Protestant Union">Protestant Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholic_League_(German)" title="Catholic League (German)">Catholic League (German)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Thirty Years War">Thirty Years War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HolyRomanEmpire_1618.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/HolyRomanEmpire_1618.png/250px-HolyRomanEmpire_1618.png" width="250" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/HolyRomanEmpire_1618.png/375px-HolyRomanEmpire_1618.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/HolyRomanEmpire_1618.png/500px-HolyRomanEmpire_1618.png 2x" data-file-width="2363" data-file-height="2362" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HolyRomanEmpire_1618.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Religion in the Holy Roman Empire on the eve of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Holy_Roman_Empire_1648.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Holy_Roman_Empire_1648.svg/250px-Holy_Roman_Empire_1648.svg.png" width="250" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Holy_Roman_Empire_1648.svg/375px-Holy_Roman_Empire_1648.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Holy_Roman_Empire_1648.svg/500px-Holy_Roman_Empire_1648.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2283" data-file-height="1810" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Holy_Roman_Empire_1648.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Empire after the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a>, 1648</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Charles V continued to battle the French and the Protestant princes in Germany for much of his reign. After his son Philip married Queen Mary of England, it appeared that France would be completely surrounded by Habsburg domains, but this hope proved unfounded when the marriage produced no children. In 1555, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_IV" title="Pope Paul IV">Paul IV</a> was elected pope and took the side of France, whereupon an exhausted Charles finally gave up his hopes of a world Christian empire. He abdicated and divided his territories between Philip and Ferdinand of Austria. The <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Augsburg" title="Peace of Augsburg">Peace of Augsburg</a> ended the war in Germany and accepted the existence of the Protestant princes, although not <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anabaptism" title="Anabaptism">Anabaptism</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Zwinglianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Zwinglianism">Swiss Reformed</a>.</p>
<p>Germany would enjoy relative peace for the next six decades. On the eastern front, the Turks continued to loom large as a threat, although war would mean further compromises with the Protestant princes, and so the Emperor sought to avoid it. In the west, the Rhineland increasingly fell under French influence. After the Dutch revolt against Spain erupted, the Dutch effectively ceased be a nominal part of the Holy Roman Empire, the culmination of a <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Burgundian_and_Habsburg_acquisitions_in_the_Low_Countries" title="Timeline of Burgundian and Habsburg acquisitions in the Low Countries">process of political divergence</a> which had already begun in <a href="/wiki/1384" title="1384">1384</a>.</p>
<p>After Ferdinand died in 1564, his son Maximilian II became Emperor, and like his father, accepted the existence of Protestantism and the need for occasional compromise with it. Maximilian was succeeded in 1576 by <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Rudolf II, Holy Roman Emperor">Rudolf II</a>, a strange man who preferred classical Greek philosophy to Christianity and lived an isolated existence in Bohemia. He became afraid to act when the Catholic Church was forcibly reasserting control in Austria and Hungary, and the Protestant princes became upset over this. Imperial power sharply deteriorated by the time of Rudolf's death in 1612. When Bohemians rebelled against the Emperor, the immediate result was the series of conflicts known as the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> (1618–48), which devastated the Empire. Foreign powers, including France and Sweden, intervened in the conflict and strengthened those fighting Imperial power, but also seized considerable territory for themselves. The long conflict so bled the Empire that it never recovered its strength.</p>
<p>The actual end of the empire came in several steps. The <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a> in 1648, which ended the Thirty Years' War, gave the territories almost complete independence. The <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Confederation" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss Confederation">Swiss Confederation</a>, which had already established quasi-independence in 1499, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Netherlands">Northern Netherlands</a>, left the Empire. The <a href="/wiki/Habsburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Habsburg">Habsburg</a> Emperors focused on consolidating their own estates in Austria and elsewhere.</p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vienna" title="Battle of Vienna">Battle of Vienna</a> (1683), the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Army of the Holy Roman Empire">Army of the Holy Roman Empire</a>, led by the Polish King <a href="/wiki/John_III_Sobieski" title="John III Sobieski">John III Sobieski</a>, decisively defeated a large Turkish army, stopping the western Ottoman advance and leading to the eventual dismemberment of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in Europe. The army was half forces of the <a href="/wiki/Poland-Lithuania_Commonwealth" class="mw-redirect" title="Poland-Lithuania Commonwealth">Poland-Lithuania Commonwealth</a>, mostly cavalry, and half forces of the Holy Roman Empire (German/Austrian), mostly infantry.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_period">Modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/18th_century_history_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="18th century history of Germany">18th century history of Germany</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prussia_and_Austria">Prussia and Austria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Prussia and Austria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Austria%E2%80%93Prussia_rivalry" title="Austria–Prussia rivalry">Austria–Prussia rivalry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Kingdom of Prussia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">Habsburg Monarchy</a></div>
<p>By the rise of <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a>, the Habsburgs were chiefly dependent on their <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Monarchy" title="Habsburg Monarchy">hereditary lands</a> to counter the rise of <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>; some of whose territories lay inside the Empire. Throughout the 18th century, the Habsburgs were embroiled in various European conflicts, such as the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Spanish_Succession" title="War of the Spanish Succession">War of the Spanish Succession</a>, the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Polish_Succession" title="War of the Polish Succession">War of the Polish Succession</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Austrian_Succession" title="War of the Austrian Succession">War of the Austrian Succession</a>. The <a href="/wiki/German_dualism" class="mw-redirect" title="German dualism">German dualism</a> between <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> dominated the empire's history after 1740.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Revolutionary_Wars_and_final_dissolution">French Revolutionary Wars and final dissolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: French Revolutionary Wars and final dissolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
